Annex 6
Circular No.: 95/11
My No. 4/Tst 1/Mic.
Ministry of Education & Higher Education,
"lsurupaya", Battaramulla.
31.03.1995
National Policy on Teachers Transfers
The Goverrunent has decided to formulate a National Policy on Teachers Transfers with a view to
ensuring the implementation of the educational activities meaningfully and providing for the maximum welfare
and benefit of the student and teacher population. The Cabinet of Ministers has decided that the National Policy
should be based on the following criteria:
1. Since education should be pupil centered the needs of the pupils should be given priority on all occasions.
2. Since the improvement of the quality of education depends on the knowledge and the skills of the teachers,
the policy on teachers transfers should promote the development of skills and knowledge in teachers.
3. The policy on teachers transfers should be formulated in a manner to ensure the professional security,
academic freedom and the job satisfaction of the teachers and should provide for the teachers to serve with a
considerable degree of confidence without the fear of being transferred out unjustifiably.
4. One of the main objectives of this policy should be to minimize the regional discrepancies observed in the
process of education.
5. The policy on transfers should ensure an equitable distribution of the human resources in the field of
education so as to make such scarce resources available to the public at the periphery.
6. Such policy should lead to the maximum utilization of the scarce human resource available in the sector
through a proper management of resources.
7. Other than in the instances where a transfer is made to facilitate the procedure when disciplinary action is
being taken or is being contemplated against a teacher, all transfers of teachers should be made on a
recommendation made by a duly constituted teachers transfer board.
8. Transfers of teachers should be effected only on the recommendations of a duly constituted transfer board
other than in the cases where disciplinary action is being take or is being contemplated against a teacher and
where transfers are made on a request by a teacher for reasons of ill-health and on exigencies of service. The
transfer boards should be informed of any transfers made on exigencies of service within a period of 2 weeks
from the date of the letter effecting the transfer.
9. Teachers transfer boards should be established at the following levels:
*  Zonal Education Office.
*  Provincial/Additional Provincial Education Office.
*  Line Ministry. In addition to the members of the teachers transfer boards to be appointed in terms of Section
5 of Chapter Ill of Establishments Code, a representative of every Teachers Union registered under the Trade
Unions Ordinance and whose membership is serving in more than one Province in the Island should be
appointed as members of the transfer board.
10. Classification of Schools - The Schools are classified as given below:
*  Very congenial
*  Congenial
*  Non-congenial
*  Difficult
11. The line Ministry in consultation with the Provincial Ministries of Education shall classify the schools as per
above.
65



12. Time Schedule - Other than in the cases specified under para 2 above, the transfers of teachers should be
effected only at the beginning of a calendar year with a view to minimizing inconveniences caused to the students
and the teachers.
13. Calling for Applications - Applications for annual transfers of teachers should be called for between 1 st June
and 30th June of each year.
14. Meetings of the Transfers Boards - The transfers boards to consider the applications received shall meet
between 1st and 31st of August each year.
15. Collection of data and information - authorities concerned shall collect and compile information and data
relating to teachers transfers and vacancies in respect of all Schools before 31st of July each year.
16. Issue of letters - Letters on annual transfers shall be issued by 15 of September each year.
17. Appeals - Appeals shall be received during the period between 15th September to 15th October each year.
18. Decision by the Appeal Board - Appeal Board shall consider all the appeals between 15th October to 30th
November and the decision of the board shall be conveyed before 30th of November.
19. Teachers transfers may be effected for reasons of ill-health, on mutual requests and requests by teachers on
very valid grounds outside the above procedure but, within the framework laid down by the National Policy.
20. The provisions of the Chapter III of the establishments shall be applicable to all transfers of teachers other
than in the cases specified in this Circular and such provisions should be followed in all instances.
21. The implementation of the Policy on teachers transfers. The authorities should ensure that every teacher
completes the minimum period of service in a School nominated by the Standing Committee as stipulated in the
Sri Lanka Teachers Service Minute. The minimum period of service in respect of diflicult schools shall be 3
years and in respect of non-congenial schools, this period should be 4 years. The maximum period of time a
teacher should serve in a congenial school shall be 8 years and shall be 6 years in a very congenial school.
22. The application for a transfer on reasons of ill-health by a teacher, shall be accompanied by a medical
certificate of a specialist in the respective field and the clinic cards. The risk factors approved by the World
Health Organization shall be accepted as valid reasons for transfers on reasons of ill-health in the case of
expectant mothers. In addition to the personal health of the teacher concerned, the health of the following
members of the family of the teacher may be taken into consideration.
*   Spouse.
-   Children.
*   Dependent parents if the teacher is not married
23. Whenever possible the transfers to the officers of the Sri Lanka Principals' Service and the Sri Lanka
Educational Administrative Service shall be made parallel to the National Policy on teachers transfers.
24. First Appointment - At the recruitment of teachers the first appointment shall be make to a school in the
following two categories:
*   Difficult.
*   Non-congenial.
25. Miscellaneous - All teachers who have not so far served in a non congenial or difficult station shall be
subjected to the minimum period of service in such a school. However, teachers of over 53 years of age shall not
be so transferred.
26. All transfer boards shall prepare a manual of procedure in terms of the National Policy provided by this
Circular and shall follow such manual of procedure in all instances.
The Cabinet of Ministers at its meeting held on 18th January 1995 has approved the National Policy laid
down by this Circular and all authorities effecting teachers transfers are required to follow this policy.
66

Annex 8
SRI LANKA
TEACHER EDUCATION AND TEACHER DEPLOYMENT PROJECT
Cost-Effectiveness of Teacher Education Programs
1      Over one third (69,000) of the teachers in Sri Lanka are attending some kind of training program
and the large majority (81 percent) of these are enrolled in training through the distance education mode
Distance education and part-time programs at NIE and the universities cover about 59,000 teachers
(Figure 1).
Figure 1: Percentage of Trainees In
Teacher Training Institutions in 1994
COE 
6%
TTC
5%
Program Costs: The cost of all teacher training programs was estimated at Rs 206 million in 1994,
representing 6 percent of the education recurrent budget. Table I and Figure 2 show that NIE has the
largest share (42%) followed by Colleges of Education (COEs), Teacher Training Colleges (TTCs) and
the universities. However, the magnitude and distribution of costs become quite different with the
inclusion of additional costs such as trainees full salaries in TTCs, student stipends in COEs. The total
cost almost doubles when these expenditures are added to reach Rs. 366 million representing 10% of the
sectoral budget. The TTC share increased substantially to 48 percent and both the TTC and COE cost
amounted to 70 percent of all program costs even though they train 11 percent of all trainees, while NIE
with 24 percent of costs, trains 81 percent of the trainees (Figure 3).
Table: 1: Recurrent Cost in Different Programs of Teacher Education (Rs '000) in 1994

Recurrent Expenditure     44,619  50,888   88,919    21,934              206,360

Trainee Stipends             -    28,985      -          -                28,985
Trainee Salaries         130,539     -         -         -               130,539
Total Cost with Stipends    175,158  79,873   88,919    21,934           365,884

2.      Program Unit Cost: Cost analysis among different programns is based on unit cost estimnates of
expenditure per trainee in the teacher training institutions. It is difficult to estimate the unit cost on the
bases of equivalent full-time students as the mixture of full-time, and part-time distance education training
modies across different types of institutions precludes the determination of a uniform base. However, the
difference in the programs' unit costs is so vast that a refinement would not change the scale of such
variations.
3.      The expenditure per student in the TTCs is by far the largest followed by the COEs, the
universities, and then by NIE (Figure 4). The unit cost differentials are usually attributed to several
factors such as program type, duration, equipment used, staff deployment, and mode of delivery.
However, the substantially high UC of the TTCs is due to the massive cost of trainee salaries which
represents more than two thirds of the TTCs' total cost. Funding policies for institutions are unclear and
appear to bear no consistent relationship to actual enrollments or the number of staff actually required in a
given year.
Figure 4: Unit Cost of Teacher Training Institutions with and
without Trainee Salaries In 1994 (RsOOO0)
60                                    56.1,S , ,: 
40 4                                 Q--Q-: _
30-                         S             _
10 -      1.6   _ s         _        -
O-~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~.... ...S.-   
NIE       Univ      COE       TTC
4.      Cost Implications of Residential Facilities: The policy of providing residential services to all
trainees of the COEs and most of the trainees in the TTCs is very expensive. The isolated location of
teacher training institutions increases the costs of all the services and makes staffing more difficult and
70



costly. For example, expenditure related to the residential facilities in one of the COEs represented over
50% of the total institution's recurrent budget. This residential isolation also makes the necessary
integration of future teachers into the community more difficult. Residential facilities increase overhead
expenditure and require substantial resources for running costs and salaries of additional service workers.
A vast segment of the cost comes from the large number of non-academic staff and service workers in
these institutions, who are required to support and maintain these facilities. The ratio of academic to non-
academic staff in 1994 was 1:1 in the TTCs and 1:3 in the COEs.
5.      Under-utilization of the residential facilities in these institutions adds to the overall costs and
increases their unit cost. In 1994, the COEs, which offer residential facilities to all their first and second
year students, only used 74% of this residential capacity (third year students do their internship year in
schools). Capacity utilization rates vary substantially among the COEs. Similar variations in capacity
utilization are found in the TTCs. Some of the institutions in remote and rural areas, which were
established under political pressure, have very low rates of utilization and are run with high operating
costs. As in other Asian countries, Sri Lanka needs to develop a plan for the relocation of teacher training
institutions to increase cost-effectiveness, reduce boarding according to needs and increase lower-cost day
students.
6.      High Cost of Low Student:Staff Ratio: The low student:staff ratio in the TTCs and the COEs
was 9:1 and 13:1, respectively, in 1994 (which declined further in 1995) increases overhead expenses and
inflates the unit cost of these institutions. In addition, the large number of non-academic staff, required
mainly for the residential services offered to the trainees, adds substantially to the recurrent costs of these
institutions. In addition, the facilities of the COEs and TTCs which carry out pre- and in-service training
programs, respectively, are under-utilized and inefficiently managed.
7.      High Cost of Trainees Salaries and Stipends: The current policy of offering payment of full
salary to the TTCs trainees and stipends to COEs students is very expensive and adds substantially to the
budget. In 1994, the teacher education system cost with student salaries and stipends (Rs 366 million) is
almost double the cost without these salaries and stipends. When salaries of trainees are added to the
training cost, the TTCs recurrent expenditure increases by almost four times and reaches Rs 175 million
and its share to the cost of all programs increases from 22% to 48%.
8.      There are no studies on assessment of the private costs of training (e.g. fees, books,
transportation, lodging). Stipends are paid universally and independently of such costs. As a result, it is
difficult to assess whether the students are receiving more or less than they actually spend for training.
The system does not include policies and measures to maximize the cost-effectiveness of the training
programs on the bases of actual cost to the Government and to the trainee.
9.      Unit Cost Variation among Institutions: There is a vast discrepancy in unit costs within TTCs
and COEs. For example, the unit cost of TTCs in the Southern Province is more than three times the unit
cost of the TTCs in North Western Province. The differences in the unit cost are partly attributed to
different level and type of equipment, facilities, and laboratories in these institutions. Institutions
specialized in math, science, and English training have different types of equipment and require
specialized teachers who are usually paid higher salaries and allowances.
10.    InceTicient Institutional Management: These discrepancies in the institutions' unit cost are
further accentuated by differences in the utilization of residential and other facilities. Training of the
COEs and TTCs management has been limited and has not focused on cost-effectiveness and how to
maximize the utilization of facilities, staff and other resources in the institution. Furthermore, the absence
of a special formula based on standard criteria for resource distribution and funding has led to the
institutions' annual budgets not reflecting their real requirements and consequently some institutions
receive more and others less than their needs. The undesirable practice of relying on untrained teachers to
fill vacancies, primarily at the secondary level, needs to be reviewed and professional criteria established
71



for entry into the service at both primary and secondary levels. The goal of the project is to establish by
year 2,000 a fully pre-service and pre-trained teacher supplied system. This has the implication that pre-
employment of teacher trainees will have to be stopped and that teachers should be employed on the basis
of demand.
11.    Within each institution, management is less effective than it should be. Selection criteria for
heads of institutions do not emphasize leadership or experience in teacher education. Training in
management is not required prior to appointment. The structure of the institutions is not conducive to
sharing responsibility or team functioning, which could be beneficial for managing such environments. If
teacher education is to become a vital force within the country, appropriate appointments of heads and
senior staff and a planned program of staff development are essential in developing strong leadership.
12.    Other Asian countries such as Indonesia, Malaysia, and the Philippines, have rationalized their
teacher training institutions on cost-effective bases by consolidation of institutions, establishment of
centralized multi-disciplinary institutions with residential service only for targeted students, contracting
some of the support services to the private sector, introducing some user fees with safety nets for the
poorer students, and maintaining a student:staff ratio that is based on full utilization of their staff
(averaging about 20:1). These measures have reduced unit costs thereby contributing to the cost-
effectiveness of the system. Sri Lanka needs to also implement such measures.
13.    Cost Effectiveness of Distance Education: The distance education program for teacher
education was introduced as a cost-effective approach to clear the backlog of untrained teachers. The
program for untrained undergraduates has been supported by SIDA. Providing there is no further massive
appointment of untrained undergraduate teachers, the backlog should be cleared by the end of 1998.
However, the trained teachers with only a certificate will need to be upgraded to diploma level. The
knowledge and experience in distance mode should be used for that purpose in the future. The backlog of
untrained graduates is currently being addressed through the universities and NIE Departments of
Teacher Education. There are no reliable data on the number of graduates who have yet to complete the
post graduate diploma program. Until the preservice P.G. Dip Ed. becomes effective, there will be a
number of untrained graduates (albeit greatly reduced) entering the service without a professional
qualification.
14.    The massive number of distance education students enrolled in NIE has resulted in a relatively
low unit cost and consequently little attention is paid to the cost of repetition and to examination
outcomes. Around 20 percent of the distance education students do not sit for the examination within the
planned time and there are no official data on what happens to those students. Monitoring as well as
follow up activities need to be strengthened.
15.    Minimum attention is given to the output cost (cost per qualified teacher) i.e. partial and
complete failure at the terminal examinations, number of students not sitting for finals, and attrition from
courses. Course drop-out represents a high cost to the sector and inflates the resources required to provide
an adequate and qualified teacher. In addition, recent impressions are that the teachers trained by means
of distance education are weaker than teachers trained at COEs in their teaching practice. However, the
distance education program has produced some very valuable training units/modules and these should not
be lost but integrated into the pre-service programs. The use of most distance training modules should be
sustained and extended to other programs through structured continuous teacher training programs.
16.    Distance Education Dependence on Foreign Financing: The distance education program at
NIM depends heavily on foreign assistance in its financing. Almost 78 percent of the Rs 66 million total
program cost is provided by SIDA. SIDA is currently financing primary basic certificate level math and
science courses. However, SIDA plans to cover only primary courses by next year and to phase out all the
program by 1998 when the major part of the backlog of untrained teachers would be cleared. SIDA will
continue to support continuing education for teachers by means of distance education.
72



17.    Complications of University Teacher Education Cost: The overall unit cost of university
education programs (Rs 4,000) is quite low compared with training in the TlCs (Rs 14,000) and COEs
(Rs 12,000). However, financial reporting of university teacher education programs is complicated by
courses being combined with other faculties and by costs of research and post-graduate courses which
make it difficult to separate cost of full-time and part-time courses and to assess equivalent full-time
student costs. It is also difficult to obtain accurate data on input for staff who are not specifically
dedicated to teacher education programs. Further, there are proportionate costs for administration, library
and other services which technically could be attributed to the recurrent cost of teacher education
programs. These factors deflate the unit cost and reduce overall costs.
18.    High Private Cost of University Teacher Education: Private costs of university programs
(which are at post-graduate level) are by far the largest in relation to other teacher training programs. For
example, students at the Faculty of Education at Colombo University pay Rs 800 per year for registration,
tuition, examination, and field work fees. Students who are enrolled in the part-time Diploma program at
the regional centers have to pay Rs 4,500 fee per year. However, the Provinces pay students back half of
that fee from the block grants they receive. These expenses bring the total private cost to about Rs 4,200
per year and this represents 7 percent of the annual average salary of the teacher.

=&=C3 )ess 141 - 50DANE                                                                April 24, 1996
Mr. Joseph Wood
Vice President
South Asia Regional Office
World Bank
Washington, D.C.
Dear Mr. Wood,
Re: Teacher Education Policy Statement
1.     The Government of Sri Lanka is requesting assistance from the International Development
Association through a Teacher Education and Teacher Deployment Project to better meet critical
teacher requirements and to upgrade the quality of education. The project will support policy
reform and related investments in the education sector. This letter outlines the Government's
policies.
2.     Sri lanka has had positive, but limited, economic growth over the past decade. At the same
time, public expenditure has risen by some 6 percent per annum in real terms between 1985-1994.
A significant proportion of the growth in public expenditure has resulted from an expansion of the
public service, together with increases in salaries. Growth in the public service has been most
marked in the teaching profession. The period 1989 to 1994 saw an increase of some 50,000
teachers, the vast majority of whom were untrained. As a result of this, the quality of the teaching
service has declined markedly, the student:teacher ratio has decreased from 28:1 in 1989 to 24:1 in
1994 at the primary level and from 22:1 to 16:1 at the secondary level. The effect of the massive
recruitment on salary expenditure was immense. Between 1989 and 1993, newly recruited teachers
added over Rs. 1.2 billion (18 percent) to the total salary expenditure. Notwithstanding the major
increase in teachers, there are still major shortages in some disciplines and an inequitable
distribution of teachers across provinces, schools, and language groups. A further problem arises
from the fact that only some 18 percent of new recruits to teaching are trained before entering the
service. While significant progress have been made in the training of untrained teachers, through
the distance mode, which relies heavily on external donor funds, it is recogrnized that this is, at
best, a remedial approach and that all teachers should be trained before entering the service.
Regrettably, there are insufficient places and resources within the present system to cater for the
initial training requirements and inadequate provision for the ongoing continuing education of
teachers in the service. Moreover, the teacher education system is fragmented and largely
unresponsive to national needs. While many institutions are overstaffed almost all are in need of
more suitable facilities and equipment and the equitable distribution of training in the country
requires improvement.
75



3.     Traditionally, Sri Lanka has been a leader in education in Asia. Participation rates to the
end of year 10, over 70 percent, are well above neighboring countries. Likewise, Sri Lanka has a
proud record in providing places for over 80 percent to grade 11. Progression to collegiate level
(years 12-13) now reaches 15 percent. Further, it is planned to extend compulsory education from
age 12 to age 16 in 1996. The significance and impact of these achievements has, however, been
diluted by the over-supply of untrained and often inappropriately assigned teachers. Apart from
the adverse impact on education quality, the additional funds required to meet the salaries of
additional teachers have had a negative impact on the provision of suitable quantity and quality of
student learning materials, equipment, furniture and general maintenance of the infrastructure. The
shortcomings have been most marked in disadvantaged and remote schools.
4.     Government has determined that there are two crucial issues which need to be urgently
addressed within the system: the rationalization of teacher deployment to improve the quality,
equity, and cost effectiveness of the system; and the restructuring and upgrading of teacher
education programs. The latter to ensure the availability of trained teachers and to provide for the
ongoing professional development of all teachers in the service. Government policies are directed
towards these interrelated goals as a priority. At the same time, given the economic outlook, it is
recognized that the issues of distributing quality educational materials, increasing equitable
allocation of resources to all parts of the country and improvements in the administration and of the
financing mechanisms of the education system should be given early attention. However, unless
the teacher issues are resolved the impact from these investments could be limited. We note that
the education budget absorbed about 11 percent of the national budget and the intent is to
maximize its use in the foreseeable future, through corrective measures that address the above
listed issues.
5.    In regard to teacher deployment, the Govenmuent's medium- to long-term objective is to
ensure that all teachers be trained before entering the teaching service. This will require a marked
increase in the number of training places. To support this initiative, cost savings will be effected
through increasing the student:teacher ratios to 26:1 for primary (grades 1-5) and 22:,1 for
secondary (grades 6-13) by 2001. It is estimated that substantial salary savings could result. In
addition to meeting part of the cost of the expansion of initial training for teachers, salary savings
would also be used to provide additional educational materials to all schools and to support
incentives-for teachers in the "non-congenial" and "difficult" schools.
6.    In order to ensure a more equitable distribution of teachers, nationally, the Govermnent
will reactivate the use of the Ready Reckoner formula, as discussed during the IDA appraisal and
ensure that all future teacher allocations are done annually and based upon that revised formula.
This will be monitored by the National Cadre Committee with representatives from the Ministry of
Education and Higher Education, the Ministry of Finance, the Finance Commission and the
provinces. It will also ensure that the recently introduced National Policy on Teacher Transfers is
implemented consistently for all teachers. This policy requires that teachers serve a minimum of 4
years in "non-congenial" and 3 years in "difficult" schools. Moreover, the maximum period that
teachers can spend in a "very congenial" and a "congenial" schools is 6 and 8 years, respectively.
As teacher salaries have improved, Government intends to reduce teacher absenteeism and will
initiate ways to relate unsatisfactory attendance to salary payment.
7.     As part of the Government's policy on the restructuring of expenditure on salaries,
financial allocations to provinces will be related directly to cadre entitlements, calculated on the
76



basis of the staffing formulae, rather than the number of staff actually employed from year to year.
Staffing entitlements and budgetary allocations will be regulated through a representative Cadre
Committee. Provinces which are overstaffed will be required to reduce the number of teachers to
accord with the approved cadre. Provinces which are understaffed will be provided with increased
funding as additional teachers are recruited. The adjustments will be made over a three-year period
beginning 1997, with the target student:teacher ratios being in place in all provinces by year 2001.
8.    In regard to the goal of restructuring and upgrading of teachers, the Government will
implement a number of policy initiatives. The first initiative involves major changes to the
structure and organization of teacher education, to be addressed on a national level. A National
Authority on Teacher Education (NATE) will be established, the principal functions being the
development of national policy, planning and coordination, and the systematic monitoring of
teacher education, including the accreditation of programs. NATE will be a semi-autonomous
institution under the Minister of Education and Higher Education. NATE will oversee the national
integrated system of teacher education, comprising 14 National College of Education (NCOEs), the
National Institute of Education (NIE), 4 universities and about 80 Teacher Centers (TC- about 78
type A and 3 type B). The Teacher Centers' principal function will be to service the professional
development needs of teachers in the field. Teacher Centers will be an integral part of NCOEs
with provision for the delivery of continuing education courses through the distance mode by both
NIE and universities. This new organization structure will be introduced in 1996 with the
establishment of NATE.
9.    The second major policy initiative relates to the nature and quality of teacher education
programs. As indicated above, Government will require as a basic employment criterion that all
teachers entering the service will have completed an accredited initial (pre-service) teacher training
program. It is acknowledged that from time to time it may be necessary to recruit a limited number
of untrained teachers to fill emergency vacancies. It is also acknowledged that 750 university
graduates could be recruited annually according to needs on the bases of 400 trained and 350
untrained. Even so, the recruits will only be employed, following the application of the Ready
Reckoner formula and following a minimum three-month intensive course; they will then be
required to take part-time training, principally through the distance education mode, in order to
gain their initial qualification. Qualitative improvements in the school system will be further
advanced through the provision of a comprehensive program of continuing professional
development for all teachers. This provision accords with the recently approved Teachers Service
Minute, which requires teachers to participate in planned continuing education courses on a five-
year cycle. Such courses will be developed through the training institutions and accredited
nationally through NATE, thereby facilitating transfer of credits among institutions. In order to
increase the flexibility of staffing for schools, all secondary teachers will be required to be trained
to teach one major and at least one minor subject. These policy initiatives will become effective in
1996 and will be monitored on an annual basis.
10.   The third major policy area, under teacher education, concems staffing levels and staff
development for personnel involved in teacher education. In order to maximize the use of staff
resources for teacher education, the minimum student:teacher educator ratio will be set at 15: 1,
based on equivalent full-time students. This target will be achieved by 1998 when new NCOEs are
scheduled to be operational. A program for extensive staff development for teacher educators and
teacher education managers will begin with the development of a comprehensive plan in 1996 and
will operate throughout the project. The Government's policy calls for an upgrading of academic
staff, including senior executives, over a five-year period to attain a profile of 10-15% first degree,
77



35-40% Post Graduate Diploma in Education, 45% Masters and 10% PhDs. Provision will also
be made for the upgrading of administrative and support staff in institutions. As a further strategy
for promoting quality, selection criteria relating to academic staff will be developed and applied
consistently. NATE, in collaboration with training institutions, will develop, in 1996, sets of
minimum criteria to be applied consistently thereafter during the project. The criteria will cover
the selection of teacher educators for the professional development program, the appointment of
heads of institutions, initial appointments to the Teacher Educator Service, and promotion within
the service. Staff allocations will also be directly linked to enrollments with the number of trainers
directly related to the trainee:teacher educator ratio of 15:1 and adjusted accordingly on an annual
basis.
11.    The rationalization of teacher education institutions is the fourth major policy area which the
Government will pursue to improve the quality, equity, and cost effectiveness of teacher education. With
the proposed setting up of 5 new NCOEs and over 80 TCs, the number of existing teacher education
institutions will be reduced from 31 to 19. In order to expand the number of places for initial training and
to increase access and the quality of facilities, 5 new NCOEs will be built in strategic locations. These
together with the upgrading and expansion of 9 existing institutions will give a more equitable
distribution of institutions across provinces and reduce the proportion of residential places required for
trainees by one-third. The Govermment is committed to increasing the number of non-residential places
as a means of attracting a wider range of students and of adjusting the unit cost of training. About 80
Teacher Centers will be distributed across provinces, having particular regard to demographic and access
factors to enable teachers to attend day and, where necessary, short residential continuing education
courses. The centers will be part of the network of NCOEs and will also accommodate the distance
education delivery requirements of NIE, Open University, and the Universities of Colombo, Peradeniya
and Jaffna, which will form part of the national integrated system. By refurbishing existing facilities of
the remaining Colleges of Education, Teachers Training Colleges and Field Study Centers, the need for
additional capital development will be reduced. The rationalization of institutions is targeted for
completion by 1998, when the new NCOEs are due to begin operating.
12.    The strengthening of the management of teacher education constitutes a fifth important
policy area for improving the effectiveness and efficiency of teacher education. In this area, the
Govemrnent will support two principal initiatives, the first being the determiination of unit cost of
different types of prograrns as the basis for establishing realistic budget parameters for training
institutions. Annual budgets will, therefore, reflect the range of programs offered by a given
institution and be linked to the number of students within each program. The second policy
initiative relates to increasing the managerial and financial autonomy of NCOEs, through the
allocation of an annual budget and a significant extension of the range of delegation for financial
and general management at the institutional level. The process will begin in 1996 as part of the
budget analysis, be piloted in 1997 and be applied to all institutions in 1998, with regular
monitoring and annual reporting to MEHE.
13.    Finally, Government would implement recent initiatives for rationalization of teacher
numbers and deployment within non economical circumstances. These measures would include:
(i) consolidation of smaller primary schools; (ii) training all primary teachers to teach all subjects
including English; (iii) following training, phasing out of specialist English language teachers in
smaller primary schools; ( (iv) eliminating the position of principal in schools with five teachers or
less; (v) consolidating secondary A level classes with 15 or less students in any stream; (vi)
consolidating small secondary schools with less than 35 students; (vii) undertaking special
recruitment of Tamil medium teachers on a contract basis; (viii) undertaking special recruitment of
78



university graduates according to identified subject specialist shortages; and (ix) introducing a
post-graduate Diploma in Education as an initial training course for an identified number of
graduates nnually prior to their employment as teachers. These school consolidation and English
training measures are expected to release over 6,850 teaching and administrative positions and will
accelerate the attrition and reduce the teaching force by over 25,000 teachers and administrators by
2005 and reduce the salary bill.
14.   Other key Ready Reckoner agreements regarding the supply of teachers within the
reformed teacher education system would include the following actions: (i) the intake to the
existing and new NCOEs would follow the institutions plan provided in the staff appraisal report
and take into consideration the designed capacity under the project (to match demand); (ii) the
recruitment of untrained graduate teachers would be the last resort and carried out only in
coordination with efforts to retrain untrained teachers while filling the needs on the subject basis;
(iii) a recruitment ceiling of 750 graduate teachers (made-up of 400 trained university graduates
with PG diplomas and 350 untrained university graduates for special subject needs following
prerequisite short-term training of 3 months) would be established. This will involve the gradual
increase of PG diploma and BEd graduates to reach an all trained graduate system intake of 750
by year 2003; and (iv) the intake into university post graduate diploma should be according to
subject needs identified by the provinces, consolidated by MEHE, and transmitted to the
universities by NATE.
15.   The main policy targets are outlined in the attached matrix. Government agrees to
undertake an annual review of the status of these policies during the implementation of the Teacher
Education and Teacher Deployment Project.
16.   In conclusion, the policy measures outlined above should allow Sri Lanka to improve the
quality, equity, and cost effectiveness of education in schools with minimal impact on the education
budget.
Yours sincerel
Mr. D. D. Pien-s-
Secretary
Ministry of Education and Hither Education
79
